are drawn to the transverse axis; prove that, $L$ being an extremity of
the latus rectum,
\[
Aq \ctrdot A'r : Ar \ctrdot A'q :: AC^2 : SL^2.
\]

\item[81.] If a tangent at a point $P$ meet the major axis in $T$, and the
perpendiculars from the focus and centre in $Y$ and $Z$, then
\[
TY^2 : PY^2 :: TZ : PZ.
\]

\item[82.] An ellipse slides between two lines at right angles to each
other; find the locus of its centre.

\item[83.] $TP$, $TQ$ are two tangents, and $CP'$, $CQ'$ are the radii from the
centre respectively parallel to these tangents, prove that $P'Q'$ is parallel
to $PQ$.

\item[84.] The tangent at $P$ meets the minor axis in $t$; prove that
\[
St \ctrdot PN=BC \ctrdot CD.
\]

\item[85.] If the circle, centre $t$, and radius $tS$, meet the ellipse in $Q$, and
$QM$ be the ordinate, prove that
\[
QM : PN :: BC : BC+CD.
\]

\item[86.] Perpendiculars $SY$, $S'Y'$ are let fall from the foci upon a pair
of tangents $TY$, $TY'$; prove that the angles $STY$, $S'TY'$ are equal to
the angles at the base of the triangle $YCY'$.

\item[87.] $PQ$ is the chord of an ellipse normal at $P$, $LCL'$ the diameter
bisecting it, shew that $PQ$ bisects the angle $LPL'$ and that $LP+PL'$ is
constant.

\item[88.] $ABC$ is an isosceles triangle of which the side $AB$ is equal to
the side $AC$. $BD$, $BE$ drawn on opposite sides of $BC$ and equally
inclined to it meet $AC$ in $D$ and $E$. If an ellipse is described round
$BDE$ having its axis minor parallel to $BC$, then $AB$ will be a tangent to
the ellipse.

\item[89.] If $A$ be the extremity of the major axis and $P$ any point on
the curve, the bisectors of the angles $PSA$, $PS'A$ meet on the tangent
at $P$.
%-----File: 103.png-----

\item[90.] If two ellipses intersect in four points, the diameters parallel
to a pair of the chords of intersection are in the same ratio to each
other.

\item[91.] From any point $P$ of an ellipse a straight line $PQ$ is drawn
perpendicular to the focal distance $SP$, and meeting in $Q$ the diameter
conjugate to that through $P$; shew that $PQ$ varies inversely as the
ordinate of $P$.

\item[92.] If a tangent to an ellipse intersect at right angles a tangent to
a confocal ellipse, the point of intersection lies on a fixed circle.

\item[93.] If from a point $T$ in the director circle of an ellipse tangents
$TP$, $TP'$ are drawn, the line joining $T$ with the intersection of the
normals at $P$ and $P'$ passes through $C$.

\item[94.] Through the middle point of a focal chord a straight line is
drawn at right angles to it to meet the axis in $R$; prove that $SR$ bears
to $SC$ the duplicate ratio of the chord to the diameter parallel to it, $S$
being the focus and $C$ the centre.

\item[95.] The tangent at a point $P$ meets the auxiliary circle in $Q'$ to
which corresponds $Q$ on the ellipse; prove that the tangent at $Q$ cuts
the auxiliary circle in the point corresponding to $P$.

\item[96.] If a chord be drawn to a series of concentric, similar, and
similarly situated ellipses, and meet one in $P$ and $Q$, and if on $PQ$
as diameter a circle be described meeting that ellipse again in $RS$,
shew that $RS$ is constant in position for all the ellipses.

\item[97.] An ellipse touches the sides of a triangle; prove that if one of
its foci move along the arc of a circle passing through two of the
angular points of the triangle, the other will move along the arc of a
circle through the same two angular points.

\item[98.] The normal at a point $P$ of an ellipse meets the conjugate
axis in $K$, and a circle is described with centre $K$ and passing through
the foci $S$ and $H$. The lines $SQ$, $HQ$, drawn through any point $Q$ of
this circle, meet the tangent at $P$ in $T$ and $t$; prove that $T$ and $t$ lie
on a pair of conjugate diameters.

\item[99.] If $SP$, $S'Q$ be parallel focal distances drawn towards the same
parts, the tangents at $P$ and $Q$ intersect on the auxiliary circle.

\item[100.] Having given one focus, one tangent and the eccentricity of
an ellipse, prove that the locus of the other focus is a circle.

\item[101.] $PSQ$ is a focal chord of an ellipse, and $pq$ is any parallel
chord; if $PQ$ meet in $T$ the tangent at $p$,
\[
pq : PQ :: Sp : ST.
\]

\item[102.] If an ellipse be inscribed in a quadrilateral so that one focus
is equidistant from the four vertices, the other focus must be at the
intersection of the diagonals.
%-----File: 104.png-----

\item[103.] If a pair of conjugate diameters of an ellipse be produced to
meet either directrix, prove that the orthocentre of the triangle so
formed is the corresponding focus of the curve.

\item[104.] A pair of conjugate diameters intercept, on the tangent
at either vertex, a length which subtends supplementary angles at
the foci.

\item[105.] The straight lines $TP$, $TQ$ are the tangents at the points
$P$, $Q$ of an ellipse; one circle touches $TP$ at $P$ and meets $TQ$ in $Q$
and $Q'$, and another circle touches $TQ$ at $Q$ and meets $TP$ in $P$ and
$P'$; prove that $PQ'$ and $P'Q$ are parallel, and that they are divided in
the same ratio by the ellipse.

\item[106.] If the normals at $P$ and $D$ meet in $E$, prove that $EC$ is
perpendicular to $PD$, and that the straight line joining $C$ to the
centroid of the triangle $EPD$ bisects the line joining $E$ to $T$, the point
of intersection of the tangents at $P$ and $D$.

\item[107.] A chord $PQ$, normal at $P$, meets the directrices in $K$ and $L$,
and the tangents at $P$ and $Q$ meet in $T$; prove that $PK$ and $QL$
subtend equal angles at $T$, and that $KL$ subtends at $T$ an angle which
is half the sum of the angles subtended by $SS'$ at the ends of the
chord.

\item[108.] The tangent at the point $P$ meets the directrices in $E$ and $F$;
prove that the other tangents from $E$ and $F$ intersect on the normal
at $P$.

\item[109.] If the tangent at any point meets a pair of conjugate
diameters in $T$ and $T'$, prove that $TT'$ subtends supplementary angles
at the foci.

\item[110.] $PSQ$, $PS'R$ are focal chords; prove that the tangent at $P$
and the chord $QR$ cut the major axis at equal distances from the
centre.
\end{exampleslist}
%-----File: 105.png-----

\cchapter{CHAPTER IV}

\csectiontoc{The Hyperbola}
\cheader{THE HYPERBOLA}

\csubsection{DEFINITION}

\textit{An hyperbola is the curve traced by a point which moves
in such a manner, that its distance from a given point is in a
constant ratio of greater inequality to its distance from a
given straight line.}

\cisubsection{Tracing the Curve}

\carticle{94.} Let $S$ be the focus, $EX$ the directrix, and $A$ the
vertex.

\includegraphicsmid{094a}

Then, as in Art.~\cartref{1}, any number of points on the curve
may be obtained by taking successive positions of $E$ on the
directrix.
%-----File: 106.png-----

In $SX$ produced, find a point $A'$ such that
\[
  SA' : A'X :: SA : AX,
\]
then $A'$ is the other vertex as in the ellipse, and, the
eccentricity being greater than unity, the points $A$ and $A'$
are evidently on opposite sides of the directrix.

Find the point $P$ corresponding to $E$, and let $A'E$, $PS$
produced meet in $P'$, then, if $P'K'$ perpendicular to the
directrix meet $SE$ produced in $L'$,
\[
  P'L' : P'K' :: SA' : A'X :: SA : AX,
\]
and the angle
\begin{gather*}
  P'L'S = L'SX = L'SP';  \\
  \therefore SP' = P'L'.
\end{gather*}

Hence $P'$ is a point in the curve, and $PSP'$ is a focal
chord.

Following out the construction we observe that, since
$SA$ is greater than $AX$, there are two points on the directrix,
$e$ and $e'$, such that $Ae$ and $Ae'$ are each equal to $AS$.

If $E$ coincide with $e$, the angle
\[
  QSL = LSN = ASe = AeS.
\]
\includegraphicsmid{094b}

Hence $SQ$, $AP$ are parallel, and the corresponding point
of the curve is at an infinite distance; and similarly the
curve tends to infinity in the direction $Ae'$.

Further, the angle $ASE$ is less or greater than $AES$,
according as the point $E$ is, or is not, between $e$ and $e'$.
%-----File: 107.png-----

Hence, when $E$ is below $e$, the curve lies above the axis,
to the right of the directrix; when between $e$ and $X$, below
the axis to the left; when between $X$ and $e'$, above the axis
to the left; and when above $e'$, below the axis to the right.
Hence a general idea can be obtained of the form of the
curve, tending to infinity in four directions, as in the figure
of Art.~\cartref{102}.

\csubsection{DEFINITIONS}

\indent\textit{The line $AA'$ is called the transverse axis of the hyperbola.}

\smallskip
\textit{The middle point, $C$, of $AA'$ is the centre.}

\smallskip
\textit{Any straight line, drawn through $C$ and terminated by
the curve, is called a diameter.}

\carticleplus{95.}{Prop.~I\@.} \textit{If $P$ be any point of an hyperbola, and
$AA'$ its transverse axis, and if $A'P$, and $PA$ produced, \textup{(}or
$PA$ and $PA'$ produced\textup{)} meet the directrix in $E$ and $F$, $EF$
subtends a right angle at the focus.}

\includegraphicsmid{095a}

By the theorem of Art.~\cartref{4}, $ES'$ bisects the angle $ASP'$
and $FS$ bisects $ASP$;
\[
\therefore ESF \text{ is a right angle.}
\]

$SAA'$ being a focal chord, this is a particular case of the
theorem of Art.~\cartref{6}.

\carticleplus{96.}{Prop.~II\@.} \textit{If $PN$ be the ordinate of a point $P$, and
$ACA'$ the transverse axis, $PN^2$ is to $AN \ctrdot NA'$ in a constant
ratio.}
%-----File: 108.png-----

Join $AP, A'P$, meeting the directrix in $E$ and $F$.
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{\indent Then} & PN : AN &:: EX : AX, &&\phantom{\indent Then}\\
&\text{and} & PN : A'N &:: FX : A'X;  &&\\
&& \therefore PN^2 :AN \ctrdot NA' &::EX \ctrdot FX:AX \ctrdot A'X &&\\
&&                        &::SX^2 : AX \ctrdot A'X,
\end{flalign*}
since $ESF$ is a right angle; that is, $PN^2$ is to $AN \ctrdot NA'$,
in a constant ratio.

\includegraphicsmid{095a} %repeated figure

Through $C$, the middle point of $AA'$, draw $CB$ at right
angles to the axis, and such that
\begin{flalign*}
&&BC^2 : AC^2 :: SX^2 &: AX \ctrdot A'X;&&\\
&\text{then}  &PN^2 : AN \ctrdot NA' &:: BC^2 : AC^2 &&\phantom{then}\\
&\text{or}    &PN^2 : CN^2 - AC^2 &:: BC^2 - AC^2 &&
\end{flalign*}

\subparagraph{Cor.} If $PM$ be the perpendicular from $P$ to $BC$
\begin{flalign*}
&&PM=CN, &\text{ and }  PN=CM;\\
&&\therefore  CM^2 : PM^2 &-AC^2 :: BC^2 : AC^2\\
&\text{or} &CM^2 : BC^2 &:: PM^2 - AC^2 : AC^2 &&\phantom{or}\\
&&\therefore  CM^2 + BC^2 &: BC^2 :: PM^2 : AC^2 &&\\
&\text{or} &PM^2 : CM^2 &+ BC^2 :: AC^2 : BC^2 &&
\end{flalign*}

\carticle{97.} If we describe the circle on $AA'$ as diameter, which
we may term, for convenience, \textit{the auxiliary circle}, the
rectangle $AN \ctrdot NA'$ is equal to the square on the tangent to
the circle from $N$.
%-----File: 109.png-----

Hence the preceding theorem may be thus expressed:

\textit{The ordinate of an hyperbola is to the tangent from its
foot to the auxiliary circle in the ratio of the conjugate to the
transverse axis.}

\subparagraph{Def.} \textit{If $CB'$ be taken equal to $CB$, on the other side of
the axis, the line $BCB'$ is called the conjugate axis.}

\textit{The two lines $AA'$, $BB'$ are the principal axes of the
curve.}

\textit{When these lines are equal, the hyperbola is said to be
equilateral, or rectangular.}

The lines $AA'$, $BB'$ are sometimes called major and
minor axes, but, as $AA'$ is not necessarily greater than $BB'$,
these terms cannot with propriety be generally employed.

If a point $N'$ be taken on $CA'$ produced, such that
$CN' = CN$, the corresponding ordinate $P'N' = PN$, and
therefore it follows that the curve is symmetrical with regard
to $BCB'$, and that there is another focus and directrix,
corresponding to the vertex $A'$.

\carticleplus{98.}{Prop.~III.\@} \textit{If $ACA'$ be the transverse axis, $C$ the
centre, $S$ one of the foci, and $X$ the foot of the directrix},
\begin{flalign*}
&&CS : CA :: CA &: CX :: SA : AX,\\
&\text{and}&CS : CX &:: CS^2 : CA^2.&&\phantom{and}
\end{flalign*}

Interchanging the positions of $S$ and $X$ for a new
\includegraphicsmid{098a}
figure, the proof of these relations is identical with the
proof given for the ellipse in Art.~\cartref{61}.

\carticleplus{99.}{Prop.~IV.\@} \textit{If $S$ be a focus, and $B$ an extremity
of the conjugate axis},
\[
BC^2 = AS \ctrdot SA',\text{ and } SC^2 = AC^2 + BC^2.
\]
%-----File: 110.png-----

Referring to Art.~(\cartref{98}), $SX = SA + AX$;
\begin{align*}
\therefore SX : AX &:: SA + AX : AX,\\
&:: SC + AC : AC;
\end{align*}
and similarly
\begin{gather*} %[F2: this really needs something like the flalign trick]
SX : A'X :: SC-AC : AC;\\
\therefore SX^2 : AX \ctrdot A'X :: SC^2 - AC^2 : AC^2.
\intertext{But}
BC^2 : AC^2 :: SX^2 : AX \ctrdot A'X;\\
\therefore BC^2 = SC^2 - AC^2 = AS \ctrdot SA'.
\end{gather*}

Hence
\[
SC^2 = AC^2 + BC^2 = AB^2;
\]
\textit{i.e.} $SC$ is equal to the line joining the ends of the axes.

\carticleplus{100.}{Prop.~V.\@} \textit{The difference of the focal distances of
any point is equal to the transverse axis.}

For, if $PKK'$, perpendicular to the directrices, meet
them in $K$ and $K'$,
\begin{flalign*}
&&S'P : PK' &:: SA : AX,\\
&\text{and}&SP : PK &:: SA : AX;&&\phantom{and}\\
&&\therefore S'P-SP : KK' &:: SA : AX,\\
&&&:: AA' : XX' \text{~(Art.~\cartref{98})};\\
&&\therefore S'P-SP &= AA'.
\end{flalign*}
\begin{flalign*}
&\calignedcor{Cor.~1.}&SP : NX &:: AC : CX;\\
&&\therefore SP : AC &:: NX : CX;\\
&&\therefore SP + AC : AC &:: CN : CX,\\
&\text{or}&SP + AC : CN &:: SA : AX.\\
&\indent\text{Hence also}&S'P-AC : CN &:: SA : AX.&&\phantom{\indent Hence also}
\end{flalign*}

\subparagraph{Cor.~2.} \textit{Hence also it can be easily shewn, that the
difference of the distances of any point from the foci of an
hyperbola, is greater or less than the transverse axis, according
as the point is within or without the concave side of the
curve.}
%-----File: 111.png-----

\carticle{101.} \textit{Mechanical Construction of the Hyperbola.}

\includegraphicsmid{101a}

Let a straight rod $S'L$ be moveable in the plane of the
paper about the point $S'$. Take a piece of string, the
length of which is less than that of the rod, and fasten one
end to a fixed point $S$, and the other end to $L$; then, pressing
a pencil against the string so as to keep it stretched, and a
part of it $PL$ in contact with the rod, the pencil will trace
out on the paper an hyperbola, having its foci at $S$ and $S'$,
and its transverse axis equal to the difference between
the length of the rod and that of the string.

This construction gives the right-hand branch of the
curve; to trace the other branch, take the string longer
than the rod, and such that it exceeds the length of the rod
by the transverse axis.

We may remark that by taking a longer rod $MS'L$, and
taking the string longer than $SS' + S'L$, so that the point $P$
will be always on the end $S'M$ of the rod, we shall obtain
an ellipse of which $S$ and $S'$ are the foci. Moreover, remembering
that a parabola is the limiting form of an ellipse
when one of the foci is removed to an infinite distance,
the mechanical construction given for the parabola will be
seen to be a particular case of the above.

\cisubsection{The Asymptotes}

\carticle{102.} We have shewn in Art.~\cartref{94} that if two points, $e$
and $e'$, be taken on the directrix such that
\[
Ae = Ae' = AS,
\]
the lines $eA$, $e'A$ meet the curve at an infinite distance.
%-----File: 112.png-----

These lines are parallel to the diagonals of the rectangle
formed by the axes, for
\begin{align*}
Ae': AX:: AS: AX&:: SC : AC,\\
                &:: AB: AC, \;\text{(Art.~\cartref{99}).}
\end{align*}

\subparagraph{Definition.} \textit{The diagonals of the rectangle formed by
the principal axes are called the asymptotes.}

\includegraphicsmid{102a}

We observe that the axes bisect the angles between the
asymptotes, and that if a double ordinate, $PNP'$, when
produced, meet the asymptotes in $Q$ and $Q'$,
\[
PQ=P'Q'.
\]

The figure appended will give the general form of the
curve and its connection with the asymptotes and the
auxiliary circle.

\carticleplus{103.}{Prop.~VI\@.} \textit{The asymptotes intersect the directrices
in the same points as the auxiliary circle, and the lines joining
the corresponding foci with the points of intersection are
tangents to the circle.}

If the asymptote $CL$ meet the directrix in $D$, joining
$SD$ (fig. Art.~\cartref{102}), $CL^2 = AC^2 + BC^2 = SC^2$,
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{and}& CD: CX:: CL: CA &:: SC: CA:: CA:CX;&&
\end{flalign*}

$\therefore CD= CA$, and $D$ is on the auxiliary circle.
%-----File: 113.png-----

Also
\[
CS \ctrdot CX = CA^2 = CD^2;
\]\indent
$\therefore CDS$ is a right angle, and $SD$ is the tangent at $D$.

\subparagraph{Cor.} $CD^2 + SD^2 = CS^2 = AC^2 + BC^2 \;\text{~(Art.~\cartref{99});}$
\[
\therefore SD = BC.
\]

\begin{small}
\carticle{104.} An asymptote may also be characterized as the ultimate
position of a tangent when the point of contact is removed to an infinite
distance.

It appears from Art.~\cartref{10} that in order to find the point of contact of
a tangent drawn from a point $T$ in the directrix, we must join $T$ with
the focus $S$, and draw through $S$ a straight line at right angles to $ST$;
this line will meet the curve in the point of contact.

In the figures of Arts.~\cartref{94} and \cartref{102} we know that the line through $S$,
parallel to $eA$ or $CL$, meets the curve in a point at an infinite distance,
and also that this straight line is at right angles to $SD$, since $SD$ is at
right angles to $CD$. Hence the tangent from $D$, that is the line from
$D$ to the point at an infinite distance, is perpendicular to $DS$ and therefore
coincident with $CD$.

The asymptotes therefore touch the curve at an infinite distance.

\end{small}

\carticleplus{105.}{Def.} \textit{If an hyperbola be described, having for its
transverse and conjugate axes, respectively, the conjugate and
transverse axes of a given hyperbola, it is called the conjugate
hyperbola.}

It is evident from the preceding article that the conjugate
hyperbola has the same asymptotes as the original
hyperbola, and that the distances of its foci from the centre
are also the same.

The relations of Art.~\cartref{96} and its Corollary are also true,
\textit{mutatis mutandis}, of the conjugate hyperbola; thus, if $R$ be
a point in the conjugate hyperbola,
\begin{flalign*}
&&RM^2 : CM^2 - BC^2 &:: AC^2 : BC^2,\\
&\text{and}& CM^2 : RM^2 + AC^2 &:: BC^2 : AC^2.&\phantom{and}
\end{flalign*}

\subparagraph{Def.} \textit{A straight line drawn through the centre and terminated
by the conjugate hyperbola is also called a diameter
of the original hyperbola.}
%-----File: 114.png-----

\carticleplus{106.}{Prop.~VII\@.} \textit{If from any point $Q$ in one of the
asymptotes, two straight lines $QPN$, $QRM$ be drawn at right
angles respectively to the transverse and conjugate axes, and
meeting the hyperbola in $P$, $p$, and the conjugate hyperbola
in $R$, $r$,}
\begin{flalign*}
&&QP \ctrdot Qp &= BC^2,\\
&\text{and}& QR \ctrdot Qr &= AC^2.&\phantom{and}
\end{flalign*}
\includegraphicsmid{106a}
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{For}&      QN^2 : BC^2 &:: CN^2 : AC^2;&\phantom{\indent\text{For}}\\
&&\therefore QN^2 - BC^2 : BC^2 &:: CN^2 - AC^2 : AC^2\\
&&&:: PN^2 : BC^2;\\
&&\therefore QN^2 - BC^2 &= PN^2,\\
&\text{or}&\quad QN^2 - PN^2 &= BC^2;&\phantom{\text{or}}\\
&\textit{i.e.}&\quad QP \ctrdot Qp &=BC^2.&\phantom{textit{i.e.}}\\
&\indent\text{Similarly,}& QM^2 : AC^2 &:: CM^2 : BC^2;&\phantom{\indent\text{Similarly}}\\
&&\therefore QM^2 - AC^2 : AC^2 &:: CM^2 - BC^2 : BC^2,\\
&&&:: RM^2 : AC^2;\\
&&\therefore QM^2 - RM^2 &= AC^2,\\
&\text{or}& QR \ctrdot Qr &= AC^2.&\phantom{\text{or}}
\end{flalign*}

These relations may also be given in the form,
\[
QP \ctrdot Pq = BC^2,\quad     QR \ctrdot Rq' = AC^2.
\]

\subparagraph{Cor.} If the point $Q$ be taken at a greater distance
from $C$, the length $QN$ and therefore $Qp$ will be increased,
and may be increased indefinitely.
%-----File: 115.png-----

But the rectangle $QP \ctrdot Qp$ is of finite magnitude; hence
$QP$ will be indefinitely diminished, and the curve, therefore,
as it recedes from the centre, tends more and more nearly to
coincide with the asymptote.

A further illustration is thus given of the remarks in
Art.~\cartref{104}.

\carticle{107.} If in the preceding figure the line $Qq$ be produced
to meet the conjugate hyperbola in $E$ and $e$, it can be shewn,
in the same manner as in Art.~\cartref{106}, that
\[
QE \ctrdot Qe = BC^2;
\]
and this equality is still true when the line $Qq$ lies between
$C$ and $A$, in which case $Qq$ does not meet the hyperbola.

\vspace{-2ex} %reduce spacing before subsection heading, so diagram fits on page
\cisubsection{Properties of the Tangent and Normal}

\carticle{108.} In the case of the hyperbola the theorem, proofs
of which are given in Arts.~\cartref{15} and \cartref{16}, takes the following
form:

\textit{The tangents drawn from any point to an hyperbola
subtend equal or supplementary angles at either focus according
as they touch the same or opposite branches of the
curve.}

\includegraphicsmid{108a}

For, $T$ being the point of intersection of tangents to
opposite branches of the curve, let $TM$, $TM'$ be the perpendiculars
%-----File: 116.png-----
let fall from $T$ on $SP$ and $SQ$, then, as in Arts.~\cartref{15}
and \cartref{16}, $TM = TM'$;

$\therefore$ the angles $TSM$, $TSM'$ are equal, and consequently
the angles $TSP$, $TSQ$ are supplementary.

\carticleplus{109.}{Prop.~VIII\@.} \textit{The tangent at any point bisects the
angle between the focal distances of that point, and the normal
is equally inclined to the focal distances.}

Let the normal at $P$ meet the axis in $G$.

Then (Art.~\cartref{18}),
\begin{flalign*}
&&              SG : SP &:: SA : AX, \\
&\text{and}&
              S'G : S'P &:: SA : AX; &\phantom{\text{and}}\\
&&  \therefore SG : S'G &:: SP : S'P;
\end{flalign*}
and therefore the angle between $SP$ and $S'P$ produced is
bisected by $PG$.

Hence $PT$, the tangent which is perpendicular to $PG$,
bisects the angle $SPS'$.

\subparagraph{Cor.~1.} If $PT$ and $GP$ produced meet, respectively,
the conjugate axis in $t$ and $g$, it can be shewn, in exactly the
same manner as in the corresponding case of the ellipse
(Art.~\cartref{68}), that $S$, $P$, $S'$, $t$, and $g$ are concyclic.

\subparagraph{Cor.~2.} If an ellipse be described having $S$ and $S'$ for
its foci, and if this ellipse meet the hyperbola in $P$, the
normal at $P$ to the ellipse bisects the angle $SPS'$, and therefore
coincides with the tangent to the hyperbola.

\textit{Hence, if an ellipse and an hyperbola be confocal, that is,
have the same foci, they intersect at right angles.}

\carticleplus{110.}{Prop.~IX\@.} \textit{Every diameter is bisected at the centre,
and the tangents at the ends of a diameter are parallel.}

Let $PCp$ be a diameter, and $PN$, $pn$ the ordinates.
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{Then}&
CN^2 : Cn^2 &:: PN^2 : pn^2,&\phantom{\indent\text{Then}} \\
&&              &:: CN^2 - AC^2 : Cn^2 - AC^2;
\end{flalign*}
hence   $CN = Cn$,  and  $\therefore CP = Cp$.
%-----File: 117.png-----

Again, if $PT$, $pt$ be the tangents,

The triangles $PCS$, $pCS'$ are equal in all respects, and
therefore $SPS'p$ is a parallelogram.

\includegraphicsmid{110a}

Hence the angles $SPS'$, $SpS'$ are equal, and therefore
$SPT = S'pt$.

But $SPC = S'pC$,

$\therefore$ the difference $TPC=\text{the difference } tpC$, and $PT$ is
parallel to $pt$.

It can be shewn in exactly the same manner, that, if
the diameter be terminated by the conjugate hyperbola, it
is bisected in $C$, and the tangents at its extremities are
parallel.

\subparagraph{Cor\@.} The distances $SP$, $Sp$ are equally inclined to the
tangents at $P$ and $p$.

\carticleplus{111.}{Prop.~X\@.} \textit{The perpendiculars from the foci on any
tangent meet the tangent on the auxiliary circle, and the
semi-conjugate axis is a mean proportional between their
lengths.}

Let $SY$, $S'Y'$ be the perpendiculars, and let $SY$ produced
meet $S'P$ in~$L$.

\includegraphicsmid{111a}

Then the triangles $SPY$, $LPY$ are equal in all respects,
\[
\text{and }SY = LY.
\]
%-----File: 118.png-----

Hence, $C$ being the middle point of $SS'$ and $Y$ of $SL$, $CY$
is parallel to $S'L$, and $S'L = 2CY$.
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{But}&  S'L = S'P - PL &= S'P - SP = 2AC; &\phantom{\indent\text{But}} \\
&&\therefore  CY &= AC,
\end{flalign*}
and $Y$ is on the auxiliary circle.

So also $Y$' is a point in the circle.

Let $SY$ produced meet the circle in $Z$, and join $Y'Z$;
then, $Y'YZ$ being a right angle, $ZY'$ is a diameter and
passes through $C$. Hence, the triangles $SCZ$, $S'CY'$ being
equal,
\begin{flalign*}
&& S'Y' &= SZ, \\
&\text{and}&   SY \ctrdot S'Y' = SY \ctrdot SZ
    &= SA \ctrdot SA' = BC^2.&\phantom{\text{and}}
\end{flalign*}

\subparagraph{Cor.~1\@.} If $P'$ be the other extremity of the diameter
$PC$, the tangent at $P'$ is parallel to $PY$, and therefore $Z$
is the foot of the perpendicular from $S$ on the tangent
at $P'$.

\subparagraph{Cor.~2\@.} If the diameter $DCD'$, drawn parallel to the
tangent at $P$, meet $S'P$, $SP$ in $E$ and $E'$, $PECY$ is a
parallelogram;
\begin{flalign*}
&&\therefore PE&=CY=AC,\\
&\text{and so also}& PE'&=CY'=AC. &\phantom{\text{and so also}}
\end{flalign*}
%-----File: 119.png-----
\carticleplus{112.}{Prop.~XI\@.} \textit{To draw tangents to an hyperbola from
a given point.}

The construction of Art.~\cartref{17} may be employed, or, as in
the cases of the ellipse and parabola, the following.

\includegraphicsmid{112a}

Let $Q$ be the given point; join $SQ$, and upon $SQ$ as
diameter describe a circle intersecting the auxiliary circle
in $Y$ and $Y'$;
\[
QY \text{ and } QY' \text{ are the required tangents.}
\]

Producing $SY$ to $L$, so that $YL = SY$, draw $S'L$ cutting
$QY$ in $P$, and join $SP$.

The triangles $SPY$, $LPY$ are equal in all respects,
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{and}& &S'P - SP = S'L = 2CY = 2AC; &\phantom{and}\\
&&           &\therefore P \text{ is a point on the hyperbola.}
\end{flalign*}

Also $QP$ bisects the angle $SPS'$, and is therefore the
tangent at $P$. A similar construction will give the other
tangent $QP'$.

If the point $Q$ be within the angle formed by the asymptotes,
the tangents will both touch the same branch of the
curve; but if it lie within the external angle, they will touch
opposite branches.
%-----File: 120.png-----

\carticleplus{113.}{Prop.~XII\@.} \textit{If two tangents be drawn from any
point to an hyperbola they are equally inclined to the focal
distances of that point.}

Let $PQ$, $P'Q$ be the tangents, $SY$, $S'Y'$, $SZ$, $S'Z'$ the
perpendiculars from the foci; join $YZ$, $Y'Z'$.

\includegraphicsmid{113a}

Then the angles $YSZ$, $Y'S'Z'$ are equal, for they are the
supplements of $YQZ$, $Y'QZ'$.
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{Also} & \phantom{\text{ (Art.~\cartref{111})};}
   SY \ctrdot S'Y' &= SZ \ctrdot S'Z' \text{ (Art.~\cartref{111})};  & \phantom{\indent\text{Also}}\\
&\text{or} & SY: SZ &:: S'Z': S'Y';
\end{flalign*}
\begin{center}
$\therefore$ the triangles $YZS$, $Y'S'Z'$ are similar,\\
and the angle $YZS = Z'Y'S'$.\\
But the angle $YQS = YZS$, and $Z'QS' = ZY'S'$;\\
$\therefore YQS=Z'QS'$.
\end{center}

That is, the tangent $QP$ and the tangent $P'Q$ produced
are equally inclined to $SQ$ and $S'Q$.

Or, producing $S'Q$, $QP$ and $QP'$ are equally inclined to
$QS$ and $S'Q$ produced.

In exactly the same manner it can be shewn that if
$QP$, $QP'$ touch opposite branches of the curve the angles
$PQS$, $P'QS'$ are equal.

\subparagraph{Cor.} If $Q$ be a point in a confocal hyperbola, the normal
at $Q$ bisects the angle between $SQ$ and $S'Q$ produced
and therefore bisects the angle $PQP'$.
%-----File: 121.png-----

\textit{Hence, if from any point of an hyperbola tangents be
drawn to a confocal hyperbola, these tangents are equally
inclined to the normal or the tangent at the point, according
as it lies within or without that angle formed by the asymptotes
of the confocal which contains the transverse axes.}

\carticleplus{114.}{Prop.~XIII\@.} \textit{If $PT$, the tangent at $P$, meet the
transverse axis in $T$, and $PN$ be the ordinate,}
\[
CN \ctrdot CT=AC^2
\]

Let fall the perpendicular $SY$ upon $PT$, and join $YN$,
$CY$, $SP$, and $S'P$.
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{The angle}& CYT &=S'PY=SPY &
    \phantom{\indent\text{The angle}} \\
&&&=\text{the supplement of }SNY=CNY;
\end{flalign*}
\includegraphicsright[10]{114a}
\noindent also the angle $YCT$ is common
to the two triangles
$CYT$, $CYN$; these triangles
are therefore similar,
and
\[
CN:CY::CY:CT,
\]
or
\[
CN \ctrdot CT=CY^2 = AC^2
\]

\begin{flalign*}
&\calignedcor{Cor.~1\@.} \text{Hence} &
    CN \ctrdot NT &=CN^2 - CN \ctrdot CT &&
    \phantom{\calignedcor{Cor.~1.} \text{Hence}}\\
&&            &=CN^2 - AC^2      \\
&&            &=AN \ctrdot NA'
\end{flalign*}

\subparagraph{Cor.~2\@.} Hence also it follows that

If any number of hyperbolas be described having the
same transverse axis, and an ordinate be drawn cutting
the hyperbolas, the tangents at the points of section will all
meet the transverse axis in the same point.

\subparagraph{Cor.~3\@.} If $CN$ be increased indefinitely, $CT$ is diminished
indefinitely, and the tangent ultimately passes
through $C$, as we have already shewn in Art.~\cartref{104}\@.
%-----File: 122.png-----

\carticleplus{115.}{Prop.~XIV\@.} \textit{If the tangent at $P$ meet the conjugate
axis in $t$, and $PN$ be the ordinate,}
\[
Ct \ctrdot PN=BC^2.
\]
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{For}& Ct : PN &:: CT : NT; \text{ (Fig.~Art.~\cartref{114})} & \phantom{\indent\text{For}} \\
&&\therefore Ct \ctrdot PN : PN^2 &:: CT \ctrdot CN : CN \ctrdot NT\\
&&&:: AC^2 : AN \ctrdot NA'.\\
&&\therefore Ct \ctrdot PN : AC^2 &:: PN^2 : AN \ctrdot NA'\\
&&&:: BC^2 : AC^2,\\
&\text{and}&Ct \ctrdot P&N = BC^2.
\end{flalign*}

In exactly the same manner as in Art.~\cartref{76}, it can be
shewn that
\begin{gather*}
CG \ctrdot CT = SC^2,\\
CG : CN :: SC^2 : AC^2,\quad Cg : PN :: SC^2 : BC^2,
\end{gather*}
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{and}&     NG : CN &:: BC^2 : AC^2. &\phantom{\text{and}}
\end{flalign*}

\includegraphicsright[15]{116a}

\carticleplus{116.}{Prop.~XV\@.} \textit{If the normal at $P$ meet the transverse
axis in $G$, the conjugate axis in $g$, and the diameter parallel
to the tangent at $P$ in $F$,}
\[
PF \ctrdot PG = BC^2, \text{ and } PF \ctrdot Pg = AC^2.
\]

Let $NP$, $PM$, perpendicular
to the axes, meet the diameter
$CF$ in $K$ and $L$;

Then $KNG$, $KFG$ being right
angles, $K$, $F$, $N$, $G$ are concyclic;
\begin{align*}
\therefore PF \ctrdot PG &= PK \ctrdot PN \\
 &=Ct \ctrdot PN=BC^2.
\end{align*}

Similarly $F$, $L$, $M$, $g$ are concyclic;
\[
\therefore PF \ctrdot Pg = PL \ctrdot PM= CT \ctrdot CN= AC^2.
\]
%-----File: 123.png-----
\carticleplus{117.}{Prop.~XVI\@.} \textit{If $PCp$ be a diameter, and $QV$ an
ordinate, and if the tangent at $Q$ meet the diameter $Pp$ in $T$,}
\[
CV \ctrdot CT=CP^2.
\]

Let the tangents at $P$ and $p$ meet the tangent at $Q$ in
$R$ and $r$;

\includegraphicsmid{117a}

Then the angle $SPR = Spr$ (Cor.~Art.~\cartref{110})\\
and therefore if $RN$, $rn$ be the perpendiculars on $SP$, $sp$, the
triangles $RPN$, $rpn$ are similar.

Draw $RM$, $rm$ perpendiculars on $SQ$.
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{Then}&   TR : Tr :: RP : rp &:: RN : rn, & \phantom{\indent\text{Then}}\\
&& &:: RM : rm \text{ (Cor.~Art.~\cartref{15})}\\
&& &:: RQ : rQ.
\end{flalign*}

Hence, $QV$, $RP$, and $rp$ being parallel,
\begin{flalign*}
&&  TP : Tp &:: PV : pV;\\
&&\therefore TP + Tp : Tp-TP &:: PV+pV : pV-PV,\\
&\text{or}&     2CP : 2CT &:: 2CV : 2CP, &\phantom{\text{or}}\\
&\text{or}&         CV \ctrdot C&T=CP^2.
\end{flalign*}
%-----File: 124.png-----

\carticleplus{118.}{Prop.~XVII\@.} \textit{A diameter bisects all chords parallel
to the tangents at its extremities.}

\includegraphicsmid{118a}

Let $PCp$ be the diameter, and $QQ'$ the chord, parallel to
the tangents at $P$ and $p$. Then if the tangents $TQ$, $TQ'$
at $Q$ and $Q'$ meet the tangents at $P$ and $p$, in the points
$E$, $E'$, $e$, $e'$,
\begin{gather*}
EP=E'P \text{ and } ep=e'p, \text{ (Art.~\cartref{21})} \\
\therefore\text{the point }T\text{ is on the line }Pp; \\
\text{but }TP\text{ bisects }QQ'; \\
\text{that is, the diameter }pCP\text{ produced bisects }QQ'.
\end{gather*}

\subparagraph{Def.} \textit{The line $DCd$, drawn parallel to the tangent at
$P$ and terminated by the conjugate hyperbola, that is, the
diameter parallel to the tangent at $P$, is said to be conjugate
to $PCp$.}

A diameter therefore bisects all chords parallel to its
conjugate.

\carticleplus{119.}{Prop.~XVIII\@.} \textit{If the diameter $DCd$ be conjugate
to $PCp$, then will $PCp$ be conjugate to $DCd$.}

Let the chord $QVq$ be parallel to $CD$ and be bisected in
$V$ by $CP$ produced.

Draw the diameter $qCR$, and join $RQ$ meeting $CD$
in $U$.
%-----File: 125.png-----

Then $RC= Cq$ and $QV= Vq$; $\therefore QR$ is parallel to $CP$.
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{Also}& QU : UR &:: Cq : CR, &\phantom{\indent\text{Also}} \\
&\text{and}& \therefore QU &= UR,
\end{flalign*}
that is, $CD$ bisects the chords parallel to $CP$, and $PCp$ is
therefore conjugate to $DCd$.

\includegraphicsmid{119a}

Hence, when two diameters are conjugate, each bisects
the chords parallel to the other.

\subparagraph{Def.} \textit{Chords drawn from the extremities of any diameter
to a point on the hyperbola are called supplemental chords.}

Thus, $qQ$, $QR$ are supplemental chords, and they are
parallel to $CD$ and $CP$; supplemental chords are therefore
parallel to conjugate diameters.

\subparagraph{Def.} \textit{A line $QV$, drawn from any point $Q$ of an
hyperbola, parallel to a diameter $DCd$, and terminated by the
conjugate diameter $PCp$, is called an ordinate of the diameter
$PCp$, and if $QV$ produced meet the curve in $Q'$, $QVQ'$ is the
double ordinate.}

This definition includes the two cases in which $QQ'$ may
be drawn so as to meet the same, or opposite branches of the
hyperbola.

\carticleplus{120.}{Prop.~XIX\@.} \textit{Any diameter is a mean proportional
between the transverse axis and the focal chord parallel to the
diameter.}

This can be proved as in Art.~\cartref{81}.
%-----File: 126.png-----

\cisubsection{Properties of Asymptotes}

\carticleplus{121.}{Prop.~XX\@.} \textit{If from any point $Q$ in an asymptote
$QPpq$ be drawn meeting the curve in $P$, $p$ and the other
asymptote in $q$, and if $CD$ be the semi-diameter parallel
to $Qq$,}
\[
QP \ctrdot Pq = CD^2 \textit{ and } QP =pq.
\]

Through $P$ and $D$ draw $RPr$, $DTt$ perpendicular to the
transverse axis, and meeting the asymptotes.
\includegraphicsmid{121a}
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{Then}&     QP : RP &:: CD : DT, & \phantom{\indent\text{Then}}\\
&\text{and}&      Pq : Pr &:: CD : Dt;\\
&&\therefore QP \ctrdot Pq : RP \ctrdot Pr &:: CD^2 : DT \ctrdot Dt.\\
&\indent\text{But}& RP \ctrdot Pr = BC^2 = DT &\ctrdot Dt
    \text{ (Arts.~\cartref{106} and \cartref{107}),}\\
&&\therefore QP \ctrdot P&q =CD^2.
\end{flalign*}
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{Similarly}& qp \ctrdot pQ &= CD^2;
    &\phantom{\indent\text{Similarly}}\\
&&\therefore QP \ctrdot Pq &= qp \ctrdot pQ;
\end{flalign*}
\begin{gather*}
\text{or, if } V \text{ be the middle point of } Qq,\\
QV^2 - PV^2 = QV^2 - pV^2.
\end{gather*}

Hence \hfill  $PV=pV$, and $\therefore PQ=pq$. \hfill\phantom{Hence\indent}

We have taken the case in which $Qq$ meets one branch
of the hyperbola. It may however be shewn in the same
manner that the same relations hold good for the case in
which $Qq$ meets opposite branches.
%-----File: 127.png-----

\subparagraph{Cor.} \textit{If a straight line $PP'p'p$ meet the hyperbola in
$P$, $p$, and the conjugate hyperbola in $P'$, $p'$, $PP'=pp'$.}

For, if the line meet the asymptotes in $Q$, $q$,
\begin{gather*}
QP'=p'q, \text{ and } PQ = qp;\\
\therefore PP'=pp'.
\end{gather*}

\carticleplus{122.}{Prop.~XXI\@.} \textit{The portion of a tangent which is
terminated by the asymptotes is bisected at the point of contact,
and is equal to the parallel diameter.}

$LEl$ being the tangent (Fig.~Art.~\cartref{121}), and $DCd$ the
parallel diameter, draw any parallel straight line $QPpq$
meeting the curve and the asymptotes.

Then $QP=pq$; and, if the line move parallel to itself
until it coincides with $Ll$, the points $P$ and $p$ coincide with
$E$, and $\therefore LE = El$.

Also\hfill $QP \ctrdot Pq = CD^2$, always;\hfill{\phantom{Also\indent}}
\[
\therefore LE \ctrdot El = CD^2, \text{ or }LE= CD.
\]

\cisubsection{Properties of Conjugate Diameters}

\carticleplus{123.}{Prop.~XXII\@.} \textit{Conjugate diameters of an hyperbola
are also conjugate diameters of the conjugate hyperbola, and
the asymptotes are diagonals of the parallelogram formed by
the tangents at their extremities.}

$PCp$ and $DCd$ being conjugate, let $QVq$, a double ordinate
of $CD$, meet the conjugate hyperbola in $Q'$ and $q'$.

Then\hfill $QV = Vq$, and $QQ' = qq'$ (Cor.~Art.~\cartref{121}),\hfill\phantom{Then\indent}
\[
\therefore Q'V = Vq'.
\]

That is, $CD$ bisects the chords of the conjugate hyperbola
parallel to $CP$.

Hence $CD$ and $CP$ are conjugate in both hyperbolas, and
therefore the tangent at $D$ is parallel to $CP$.
%-----File: 128.png-----

Let the tangent at $P$ meet the asymptote in $L$; then
\[
PL=CD \text{ (Art.~\cartref{122}).}
\]

\includegraphicsmid{123a}

Hence $LD$ is parallel and equal to $CP$;\\
but the tangent at $D$ is parallel to $CP$;
\[
\therefore LD \text{ is the tangent at } D.
\]

Completing the figure, the tangents at $p$ and $d$ are parallel
to those at $P$ and $D$, and therefore the asymptotes are
the diagonals of the parallelogram $Lll'L'$.

\subparagraph{Cor.} Hence, joining $PD$, it follows that $PD$ is parallel
to the asymptote $lCL'$, since $LP = PL'$, and $LD = Dl$,

\carticleplus{124.}{Prop.~XXIII\@.} \textit{If $QV$ be an ordinate of a diameter
$PCp$, and $DCd$ the conjugate diameter,}
\[
QV^2 : PV \ctrdot Vp :: CD^2 : CP^2.
\]

\includegraphicsmid{124a}

Let $QV$ and the tangent at $P$ meet the asymptote in
$R$ and $L$.
%-----File: 129.png-----

Then $LP$ being equal to $CD$,
\begin{align*}
RV^2 : CD^2 &:: CV^2 : CP^2;\\
\therefore RV^2 - CD^2 : CD^2 &:: CV^2 - CP^2 : CP^2.
\end{align*}

But\hfill $RV^2 - QV^2 = CD^2$. \hfill\phantom{But\indent}

Hence\hfill $QV^2 : CD^2 :: CV^2 - CP^2 : CP^2$, \hfill{\phantom{Hence}\indent}
\[
\text{or } QV^2 : PV \ctrdot Vp :: CD^2 : CP^2.
\]

\carticleplus{125.}{Prop.~XXIV\@.} \textit{If $QV$ be an ordinate of a diameter
$PCp$, and if the tangent at $Q$ meet the conjugate diameter,
$DCd$, in $t$,}
\[
Ct \ctrdot QV= CD^2.
\]

For, (Fig.~Art.~\cartref{118})
\begin{gather*}
Ct : QV :: CT : VT,\\
\text{and }\therefore Ct \ctrdot QV : QV^2 :: CV \ctrdot CT : CV \ctrdot VT.
\end{gather*}

But\hfill $CV \ctrdot CT= CP^2$, \hfill\phantom{But\indent}
\begin{align*}
\text{and } CV \ctrdot VT=CV^2 &- CV \ctrdot CT = CV^2 - CP^2;\\
\therefore Ct \ctrdot QV : QV^2 &:: CP^2 : CV^2 - CP^2,\\
&:: CD^2 : QV^2.
\end{align*}

Hence\hfill $Ct \ctrdot QV= CD^2$. \hfill\phantom{Hence\indent}

\carticleplus{126.}{Prop.~XXV\@.} \textit{If $ACa$, $BCb$ be conjugate diameters,
and $PCp$, $DCd$ another pair of conjugate diameters, and if
$PN$, $DM$ be ordinates of $ACa$,}
\begin{gather*}
CM : PN :: AC : BC,\\
\text{and } DM : CN :: BC : AC.
\end{gather*}

Let the tangents at $P$ and $D$ meet $ACa$ in $T$ and $t$;\\
then $CN \ctrdot CT = AC^2 = CM \ctrdot Ct$ (Art.~\cartref{117}),
\begin{align*}
\therefore CM : CN &:: CT: Ct,\\
&:: PT : CD,\\
&:: PN : DM,\\
&:: CN : Mt;
\end{align*}
%-----File: 130.png-----
\begin{flalign*}
&&\therefore CN^2 = CM \ctrdot Mt &= CM^2 + CM \ctrdot Ct = CM^2 + AC^2,\\
&\text{so that}&    CM^2 &= CN^2 - AC^2. &\phantom{\text{so that}}
\end{flalign*}
\includegraphicsmid{126a}
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{But}&   PN^2 : CN^2 - AC^2 &:: BC^2 : AC^2;
    &\phantom{\indent\text{But}}\\
&&\therefore CM : PN &:: AC : BC;
\end{flalign*}
and, similarly,\hfill    $DM : CN :: BC : AC$. \hfill\phantom{and, similarly,}

\subparagraph{Cor.} We have shewn in the course of the proof, that
\[
CN^2 - CM^2 = AC^2.
\]

Similarly, if $Pn$, $Dm$ be ordinates of $BC$,
\begin{flalign*}
&&Cm^2 &-Cn^2 = BC^2;\\
&\text{that is,}&     DM^2 &-PN^2 = BC^2; &\phantom{\text{that is,}}
\end{flalign*}
and it must be noticed that these relations are shewn for any
pair of conjugate diameters $ACa$, $BCb$, including of course
the axes.

\carticleplus{127.}{Prop.~XXVI\@.}  \textit{If $CP$, $CD$ be conjugate semi-diameters,
and $AC$, $BC$ the semi-axes},
\[
CP^2 - CD^2 = AC^2 - BC^2.
\]

For, drawing the ordinates $PN$, $DM$, and remembering
that in this case the angles at $N$ and $M$ are right angles, we
have, from the figure of the previous article,
\begin{align*}
CP^2 &=CN^2 + PN^2,\\
CD^2 &=CM^2 + DM^2.
\end{align*}

But $CN^2 - CM^2 = AC^2$ and $DM^2 - PN^2 = BC^2$;
\[
\therefore CP^2 - CD^2 = AC^2 - BC^2.
\]
%-----File: 131.png-----

\carticleplus{128.}{Prop.~XXVII\@.} \textit{If the normal at $P$ meet the axes
in $G$ and $g$,}
\begin{flalign*}
&& PG : CD &:: BC : AC,\\
&\textit{and}&  Pg : CD &:: AC : BC. & \phantom{\textit{and}}
\end{flalign*}

For the proofs of these relations, see Art.~\cartref{86}.

Observe also that
\[
PG \ctrdot Pg = CD^2,
\]
and that
\[
Gg : CD :: SC^2 : AC \ctrdot BC.
\]

\carticleplus{129.}{Prop.~XXVIII\@.} \textit{The area of the parallelogram
formed by the tangents at the ends of conjugate diameters
is equal to the rectangle contained by the axes.}

Let $CP$, $CD$ be the semi-diameters, and $PN$, $DM$ the
ordinates of the transverse axis.

\includegraphicsright[9]{129a}

Let the normal at $P$ meet
$CD$ in $F$, and the axis in $G$.
Then $PNG$, $CDM$ are similar
triangles, and, exactly as in
Art.~\cartref{87}, it can be shewn that
\[
PF \ctrdot CD=AC \ctrdot BC.
\]

Hence it follows that, in
the figure of Art.~\cartref{123}, the
triangle $LCL'$ is of constant area.

For the triangle is equal to the parallelogram $CPLD$.

\carticleplus{130.}{Prop.~XXIX\@.} \textit{If $SP$, $S'P$ be the focal distances of
a point $P$, and $CD$ be conjugate to $CP$,}
\[
SP \ctrdot S'P = CD^2.
\]

Attending to the figure of Art.~\cartref{111}, the proof is the
same as that of Art.~\cartref{88}.
%-----File: 132.png-----

\carticleplus{131.}{Prop.~XXX\@.} \textit{If the tangent at $P$ meet a pair
of conjugate diameters in $T$ and $t$, and $CD$ be conjugate
to $CP$,}
\[
PT \ctrdot Pt=CD^2.
\]

This can be proved as in Art.~\cartref{89}.

It can also be shewn that if the tangent at $P$ meet two
parallel tangents in $T'$ and $t'$,
\[
PT' \ctrdot Pt'=CD^2.
\]

\carticleplus{132.}{Prop.~XXXI\@.} \textit{If the tangent at $P$ meet the
asymptotes in $L$ and $L'$,}
\[
CL \ctrdot CL' = SC^2.
\]

\includegraphicsmid{132a}

Let the tangent at $A$ meet the asymptotes in $K$ and
$K'$; then (Art.~\cartref{129}) the triangles $LCL'$, $KCK'$ are of equal
area, and therefore
\[
CL : CK' :: CK : CL' \text{ (Euclid, Book \textsc{vi}.),}
\]
or\hfill  $CL \ctrdot CL' = CK^2 = AC^2 + BC^2 = SC^2$. \hfill\phantom{or}

\subparagraph{Cor.} If $PH$, $PH'$ be drawn parallel to, and terminated
by the asymptotes,
\[
4 \ctrdot PH \ctrdot PH' = CS^2,
\]
for $CL = 2PH'$, and $CL' = 2PH$.
%-----File: 133.png-----

\carticleplus{133.}{Prop.~XXXII\@.} \textit{Pairs of tangents at right angles
to each other intersect on a fixed circle.}

$PT$, $QT$ being two tangents at right angles, let $SY$,
perpendicular to $PT$, meet $S'P$ in $K$.

\includegraphicsmid{133a}

Then (Art.~\cartref{113}) the angle $S'TY' = QTS$,
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{and obviously, }&&  KTP = PTS; &\phantom{and obviously, }
\end{flalign*}
therefore $S'TY'$ is complementary to $KTP$, and $S'TK$ is a
right angle.

Hence
\begin{align*}
          4AC^2 &= S'K^2 = S'T^2 + TK^2  \\
                &= S'T^2 + ST^2  \\
                &= 2 \ctrdot CT^2 + 2 \ctrdot CS^2
\text{ by Euclid \textsc{ii}.~12 and 13;}  \\
\therefore CT^2 &= AC^2 - BC^2,
\end{align*}
and the locus of $T$ is a circle.

If $AC$ be less than $BC$, this relation is impossible.

In this case, however, the angle between the asymptotes
is greater than a right angle, and the angle $PTQ$ between a
pair of tangents, being always greater than the angle between
the asymptotes, is greater than a right angle. The problem
is therefore \textit{à priori} impossible for the hyperbola, but becomes
possible for the conjugate hyperbola.

As in the case of the ellipse, the locus of $T$ is called the
director circle.
%-----File: 134.png-----

\carticleplus{134.}{Prop.~XXXIII\@.} \textit{The rectangles contained by the
segments of any two chords which intersect each other are in
the ratio of the squares on the parallel diameters.}

\includegraphicsmid{134a}

Through any point $O$ in a chord $QOQ'$ draw the diameter
$ORR'$; and let $CD$ be parallel to $QQ'$, and $CP$ conjugate to
$CD$, bisecting $QQ'$ in $V$.

Draw $RU$ an ordinate of $CP$.
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{Then}&   RU^2 : CU^2 - CP^2 &:: CD^2 : CP^2;
    &\phantom{\indent\text{Then}} \\
&&\therefore CD^2 + RU^2 : CU^2 &:: CD^2 : CP^2,\\
&&&:: CD^2 + QV^2 : CV^2.\\
&\indent\text{But}&     RU^2 : CU^2 &:: OV^2 : CV^2;
\end{flalign*}
\[
\therefore CD^2 : CU^2 :: CD^2 + QV^2 - OV^2 : CV^2,
\]
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{or}& CD^2 : CD^2 + QV^2 - OV^2 &:: CU^2 : CV^2,
    &\phantom{\text{or}}\\
&&&:: CR^2 : CO^2;
\end{flalign*}
\begin{flalign*}
&&\therefore CD^2 : QV^2 - OV^2 &:: CR^2 : CO^2 - CR^2,\\
&\text{or}&  CD^2 : QO \ctrdot OQ' &:: CR^2 : OR \ctrdot OR'.
    &\phantom{\text{or}}
\end{flalign*}

Similarly, if $qOq'$ be any other chord, and $Cd$ the parallel
semi-diameter,
\begin{gather*}
Cd^2 : qO \ctrdot Oq' :: CR^2 : OR \ctrdot OR';\\
\therefore QO \ctrdot OQ' : qO \ctrdot Oq' :: CD^2 : Cd^2;
\end{gather*}
that is, the ratio of the rectangles depends only on the
directions of the chords.
%-----File: 135.png-----

\subparagraph{Prop.~XXXIV\@.} \textit{If a circle intersect an hyperbola in four
points, the several pairs of the chords of intersection are
equally inclined to the axes.}

For the proof, see Art.~\cartref{93}.

\csubsection{EXAMPLES}

\begin{exampleslist}

\item[1.] If a circle be drawn so as to touch two fixed circles externally,
the locus of its centre is an hyperbola.
\cheader{EXAMPLES}

\item[2.] If the tangent at $B$ to the conjugate meet the latus rectum in
$D$, the triangles $SCD$, $SXD$ are similar.

\item[3.] The straight line drawn from the focus to the directrix, parallel
to an asymptote, is equal to the semi-latus rectum, and is bisected by
the curve.

\item[4.] Given the asymptotes and a focus, find the directrix.

\item[5.] Given the centre, one asymptote, and a directrix, find the focus.

\item[6.] Parabolas are described passing through two fixed points, and
having their axes parallel to a fixed line; the locus of their foci is an
hyperbola.

\item[7.] The base of a triangle being given, and also the point of contact
with the base of the inscribed circle, the locus of the vertex is an
hyperbola.

\item[8.] If the normal at $P$ meet the conjugate axis in $g$, and $gN$ be the
perpendicular on $SP$, then $PN=AC$.

\item[9.] Draw a tangent to an hyperbola, or its conjugate, parallel to a
given line.

\item[10.] If $AA'$ be the axis of an ellipse, and $PNP'$ a double ordinate,
the locus of the intersection of $A'P$ and $P'A$ is an hyperbola.

\item[11.] The tangent at $P$ bisects any straight line perpendicular to
$AA'$, and terminated by $AP$, and $A'P$.

\item[12.] If $PCp$ be a diameter, and if $Sp$ meet the tangent at $P$ in $T$,
\[
SP=ST.
\]

\item[13.] Given an asymptote, the focus, and a point; construct the
hyperbola.
%-----File: 136.png-----

\item[14.] A circle can be drawn through the foci and the intersections of
any tangent with the tangents at the vertices.

\item[15.] Given an asymptote, the directrix, and a point; construct the
hyperbola.

\item[16.] If through any point of an hyperbola straight lines are drawn
parallel to the asymptotes and meeting any semi-diameter $CQ$ in $P$
and $R$,
\[
CP \ctrdot CR=CQ^2.
\]

\item[17.] $PN$ is an ordinate and $NQ$ parallel to $AB$ meets the conjugate
axis in $Q$; prove that $QB \ctrdot QB'=PN^2$.

\item[18.] $NP$ is an ordinate and $Q$ a point in the curve; $AQ$, $A'Q$ meet
$NP$ in $D$ and $E$; prove that $ND \ctrdot NE=NP^2$.

\item[19.] If a tangent cut the major axis in the point $T$, and perpendiculars
$SY$, $HZ$ be let fall on it from the foci, then
\[
AT \ctrdot A'T=YT \ctrdot ZT.
\]

\item[20.] In the tangent at $P$ a point $Q$ is taken such that $PQ$ is proportional
to $CD$; shew that the locus of $Q$ is an hyperbola.

\item[21.] Tangents are drawn to an hyperbola, and the portion of each
tangent intercepted by the asymptotes is divided in a constant ratio;
prove that the locus of the point of section is an hyperbola.

\item[22.] If the tangent and normal at $P$ meet the conjugate axis in $t$
and $K$ respectively, prove that a circle can be drawn through the foci
and the three points $P$, $t$, $K$.

Shew also that
\begin{flalign*}
&& GK : SK &:: SA : AX,\\
&\text{and}& St : tK &:: BC : CD, &\phantom{\text{and}}
\end{flalign*}
$CD$ being conjugate to $CP$.

\item[23.] Shew that the points of trisection of a series of conterminous
circular arcs lie on branches of two hyperbolas; and determine the
distance between their centres.

\item[24.] If the tangent at any point $P$ cut an asymptote in $T$, and if
$SP$ cut the same asymptote in $Q$, then $SQ=QT$.

\item[25.] A series of hyperbolas having the same asymptotes is cut by
a straight line parallel to one of the asymptotes, and through the
points of intersection lines are drawn parallel to the other, and equal
to either semi-axis of the corresponding hyperbola: prove that the
locus of their extremities is a parabola.

\item[26.] Prove that the rectangle $PY \ctrdot PY'$ in an ellipse is equal to
the square on the conjugate axis of the confocal hyperbola passing
through $P$.
%-----File: 137.png-----

\item[27.] If the tangent at $P$ meet one asymptote in $T$, and a line $TQ$
be drawn parallel to the other asymptote to meet the curve in $Q$; prove
that if $PQ$ be joined and produced both ways to meet the asymptotes
in $R$ and $R'$, $RR'$ will be trisected at the points $P$ and $Q$.

\item[28.] The tangent at a point $P$ of an ellipse meets the hyperbola
having the same axes as the ellipse in $C$ and $D$. If $Q$ be the middle
point of $CD$, prove that $OQ$ and $OP$ are equally inclined to the axes, $O$
being the centre of the ellipse.

\item[29.] Given one asymptote, the direction of the other, and the
position of one focus, determine the position of the vertices.

\item[30.] Two points are taken on the same branch of the curve, and on
the same side of the axis; prove that a circle can be drawn touching
the four focal distances.

\item[31.] Supposing the two asymptotes and one point of the curve to
be given in position, shew how to construct the curve; and find the
position of the foci.

\item[32.] Given a pair of conjugate diameters, construct the axes.

\item[33.] If $PH$, $PK$ be drawn parallel to the asymptotes from a point
$P$ on the curve, and if a line through the centre meet them in $R$, $T$,
and the parallelogram $PRQT$ be completed, $Q$ is a point on the curve.

\item[34.] The ordinate $NP$ at any point of an ellipse is produced to a
point $Q$, such that $NQ$ is equal to the sub-tangent at $P$; prove that the
locus of $Q$ is an hyperbola.

\item[35.] If a given point be the focus of any hyperbola, passing through
a given point and touching a given straight line, prove that the locus of
the other focus is an arc of a fixed hyperbola.

\item[36.] An ellipse and hyperbola are described, so that the foci of
each are at the extremities of the transverse axis of the other; prove
that the tangents at their points of intersection meet the conjugate
axis in points equidistant from the centre.

\item[37.] A circle is described about the focus as centre, with a radius
equal to one-fourth of the latus rectum; prove that the focal distances
of the points at which it intersects the hyperbola are parallel to the
asymptotes.

\item[38.] The tangent at any point forms a triangle with the asymptotes:
determine the locus of the point of intersection of the straight lines
drawn from the angles of this triangle to bisect the opposite sides.

\item[39.] If $SY$, $S'Y'$ be the perpendiculars on the tangent at $P$, a circle
can be drawn through the points $Y$, $Y'$, $N$, $C$.

\item[40.] The straight lines joining each focus to the foot of the perpendicular
from the other focus on the tangent meet on the normal and
bisect it.
%-----File: 138.png-----

\item[41.] If the tangent and normal at $P$ meet the axis in $T$ and $G$,
$NG \ctrdot CT=BC^2$.

\item[42.] If the tangent at $P$ meet the axes in $T$ and $t$, the angles $PSt$,
$STP$ are supplementary.

\item[43.] If the tangent at $P$ meet any conjugate diameters in $T$ and $t$,
the triangles $SPT$, $S'Pt$ are similar.

\item[44.] If the diameter conjugate to $CP$ meet $SP$ and $S'P$ in $E$ and
$E'$, prove that the circles about the triangles $SCE$, $S'CE'$ are equal.

\item[45.] The locus of the centre of the circle inscribed in the triangle
$SPS'$ is a straight line.

\item[46.] If $PN$ be an ordinate, and $NQ$ parallel to $AP$ meet $CP$ in $Q$,
$AQ$ is parallel to the tangent at $P$.

\item[47.] If an asymptote meet the directrix in $D$, and the tangent at
the vertex in $E$, $AD$ is parallel to $SE$.

\item[48.] The radius of the circle touching the curve and its asymptotes
is equal to the portion of the latus rectum produced, between its
extremity and the asymptote.

\item[49.] If $G$ be the foot of the normal, and if the tangent meet the
asymptotes in $L$ and $M$, $GL=GM$.

\item[50.] With two conjugate diameters of an ellipse as asymptotes, a
pair of conjugate hyperbolas is constructed: prove that if one hyperbola
touch the ellipse, the other will do so likewise; prove also that the
diameters drawn through the points of contact are conjugate to each
other.

\item[51.] If two tangents be drawn the lines joining their intersections
with the asymptotes will be parallel.

\item[52.] The locus of the centre of the circle touching $SP$, $S'P$ produced,
and the major axis, is an hyperbola.

\item[53.] If from a point $P$ in an hyperbola, $PK$ be drawn parallel to
an asymptote to meet the directrix in $K$, then $PK=SP$.

\item[54.] If $PD$ be drawn parallel to an asymptote, to meet the conjugate
hyperbola in $D$, $CP$ and $CD$ are conjugate diameters.

\item[55.] If $QR$ be a chord parallel to the tangent at $P$, and if $QL$, $PN$,
$RM$ be drawn parallel to one asymptote to meet the other,
\[
CL \ctrdot CM= CN^2.
\]

\item[56.] If a circle touch the transverse axis at a focus, and pass
through one end of the conjugate, the chord intercepted by the
conjugate is a third proportional to the conjugate and transverse
semi-axes.
%-----File: 139.png-----

\item[57.] A line through one of the vertices, terminated by two lines
drawn through the other vertex parallel to the asymptotes, is bisected
at the other point where it cuts the curve.

\item[58.] If $PSQ$ be a focal chord, and if the tangents at $P$ and $Q$ meet
in $T$, the difference between $PTQ$ and half $PS'Q$ is a right angle.

\item[59.] If a straight line passing through a fixed point $C$ meet two
fixed lines $OA$, $OB$ in $A$ and $B$, and if $P$ be taken in $AB$ such that
$CP^2 = CA \ctrdot CB$, the locus of $P$ is an hyperbola, having its asymptotes
parallel to $OA$, $OB$.

\item[60.] If from the points $P$ and $Q$ in an hyperbola there be
drawn $PL$, $QM$ parallel to each other to meet one asymptote, and
$PR$, $QN$ also parallel to each other to meet the other asymptote,
$PL \ctrdot PR=QM \ctrdot QN$.

\item[61.] Prove that the locus of the point of intersection of two
tangents to a parabola which cut at a constant angle is an hyperbola,
and that the angle between its asymptotes is double the external angle
between the tangents.

\item[62.] An ordinate $VQ$ of any diameter $CP$ is produced to meet the
asymptote in $R$, and the conjugate hyperbola in $Q'$; prove that
\[
QV^2 + Q'V^2 = 2RV^2.
\]

Prove also that the tangents at $Q$ and $Q'$ meet the diameter $CP$ in
points equidistant from $C$.

\item[63.] A chord $QPL$ meets an asymptote in $L$, and a tangent from $L$
is drawn touching at $R$; if $PM$, $RE$, $QN$, be drawn parallel to the
asymptote to meet the other,
\[
PM+QN=2 \ctrdot RE.
\]

\item[64.] Tangents are drawn from any point in a circle through the
foci; prove that the lines bisecting the angle between the tangents,
or between one tangent and the other produced, all pass through a
fixed point.

\item[65.] If a circle through the foci meet two confocal hyperbolas
in $P$ and $Q$, the angle between the tangents at $P$ and $Q$ is equal
to $PSQ$.

\item[66.] If $SY$, $S'Y'$ be perpendiculars on the tangent at $P$, and if $PN$
be the ordinate, the angles $PNY$, $PNY'$ are supplementary.

\item[67.] Find the position of $P$ when the area of the triangle $YCY'$ is
the greatest possible, and shew that, in that case,
\[
PN \ctrdot SC=BC^2.
\]

\item[68.] If the tangent at $P$ meet the conjugate axis in $t$, the areas of
the triangles $SPS'$, $StS'$ are in the ratio of $CD^2 : St^2$.
%-----File: 140.png-----

\item[69.] If $SY$, $SZ$ be perpendiculars on two tangents which meet in $T$,
$YZ$ is perpendicular to $S'T$.

\item[70.] A circle passing through a focus, and having its centre on the
transverse axis, touches the curve; shew that the focal distance of the
point of contact is equal to the latus rectum.

\item[71.] If $CQ$ be conjugate to the normal at $P$, then is $CP$ conjugate
to the normal at $Q$.

\item[72.] From a point in the auxiliary circle lines are drawn touching
the curve in $P$ and $P'$; prove that $SP$, $S'P'$ are parallel.

\item[73.] If any hyperbola is drawn confocal with a given ellipse, and
if $PN$ is the ordinate of a point of intersection of the hyperbola with
the ellipse, and $NT$ the tangent from $N$ to the auxiliary circle of the
hyperbola, prove that the angle $PNT$ is always the same.

\item[74.] Find the locus of the points of contact of tangents to a series
of confocal hyperbolas from a fixed point in the axis.

\item[75.] Tangents to an hyperbola are drawn from any point in one
of the branches of the conjugate, shew that the chord of contact will
touch the other branch of the conjugate.

\item[76.] An ordinate $NP$ meets the conjugate hyperbola in $Q$; prove
that the normals at $P$ and $Q$ meet on the transverse axis.

\item[77.] A parabola and an hyperbola have a common focus $S$ and
their axes in the same direction. If a line $SPQ$ cut the curves in $P$
and $Q$, the angle between the tangents at $P$ and $Q$ is equal to half the
angle between the axis and the other focal distance of the hyperbola.

\item[78.] If an hyperbola be described touching the four sides of a
quadrilateral which is inscribed in a circle, and one focus lie on the
circle, the other focus will also lie on the circle.

\item[79.] A conic section is drawn touching the asymptotes of an
hyperbola.  Prove that two of the chords of intersection of the
curves are parallel to the chord of contact of the conic with the
asymptotes.

\item[80.] A parabola $P$ and an hyperbola $H$ have a common focus,
and the asymptotes of $H$ are tangents to $P$; prove that the tangent
at the vertex of $P$ is a directrix of $H$, and that the tangent to $P$
at the point of intersection passes through the further vertex of $H$.

\item[81.] From a given point in an hyperbola draw a straight line
such that the segment intercepted between the other intersection
with the hyperbola and a given asymptote shall be equal to a
given line.

When does the problem become impossible?
%-----File: 141.png-----

\item[82.] If an ellipse and a confocal hyperbola intersect in $P$, an
asymptote passes through the point on the auxiliary circle of the
ellipse corresponding to $P$.

\item[83.] $P$ is a point on an hyperbola whose foci are $S$ and $H$; another
hyperbola is described whose foci are $S$ and $P$, and whose transverse
axis is equal to $SP-2PH$: shew that the hyperbolas will meet only at
one point, and that they will have the same tangent at that point.

\item[84.] $A$ point $D$ is taken on the axis of an hyperbola, of which the
eccentricity is~$2$, such that its distance from the focus $S$ is equal to
the distance of $S$ from the further vertex $A'$; $P$ being any point on the
curve, $A'P$ meets the latus rectum in $K$. Prove that $DK$ and $SP$
intersect on a certain fixed circle.

\item[85.] Shew that the locus of the point of intersection of tangents
to a parabola, making with each other a constant angle equal to half a
right angle, is an hyperbola.

\item[86.] The tangent and normal at any point intersect the asymptotes
and axes respectively in four points which lie on a circle passing
through the centre of the curve.

The radius of this circle varies inversely as the perpendicular from
the centre on the tangent.

\item[87.] The difference between the sum of the squares of the distances
of any point from the ends of any diameter and the sum of the squares
of its distances from the ends of the conjugate is constant.

\item[88.] If a tangent meet the asymptotes in $L$ and $M$, the angle
subtended by $LM$ at the farther focus is half the angle between the
asymptotes.

\item[89.] If $PN$ be the ordinate of $P$, and $PT$ the tangent, prove that
$SP : ST :: AN : AT$.

\item[90.] If an ellipse and an hyperbola are confocal, the asymptotes
pass through the points on the auxiliary circle of the ellipse which
correspond to the points of intersection of the two curves.

\item[91.] Two adjacent sides of a quadrilateral are given in magnitude
and position; if the quadrilateral be such that a circle can be inscribed
in it, the locus of the point of intersection of the other two sides is
an hyperbola.

\item[92.] The tangent at $P$ meets the conjugate axis in $t$, and $tQ$ is
perpendicular to $SP$; prove that $SQ$ is of constant length.

\item[93.] An hyperbola, having a given transverse axis, has one focus
fixed, and always touches a given straight line; the locus of the other
focus is a circle.

\item[94.] A chord $PRVQ$ meets the directrices in $R$ and $V$; shew that
$PR$ and $VQ$ subtend, each at the focus nearer to it, angles of which the
sum is equal to the angle between the tangents at $P$ and $Q$.
%-----File: 142.png-----

\item[95.] A circle is drawn touching the transverse axis of an hyperbola
at its centre, and also touching the curve; prove that the diameter
conjugate to the diameter through either point of contact is equal to
the distance between the foci.

\item[96.] A parabola is described touching the conjugate axes of an
hyperbola at their extremities; prove that one asymptote is parallel to
the axis of the parabola, and that the other asymptote is parallel to
the chords of the parabola bisected by the first.

If a straight line parallel to the second asymptote meet the hyperbola
and its conjugate in $P$, $P'$, and the parabola in $Q$, $Q'$, it may be
shewn that $PQ=P'Q'$.

\item[97.] If two points $E$ and $E'$ be taken in the normal $PG$ such that
$PE=PE'=CD$, the loci of $E$ and $E'$ are hyperbolas having their axes
equal to the sum and difference of the axes of the given hyperbola.

\item[98.] If two tangents are drawn to the same branch of an hyperbola,
the external angle between them is half the difference between the
angles which the chord of contact subtends at the foci.

If the tangents are drawn to opposite branches, the angle between
them is half the sum, or half the difference, of these angles according as
the points of contact are on the same or on opposite sides of the
transverse axis.

\item[99.] Parabolas are drawn passing through two fixed points $A$ and
$B$, and having their axes in a given direction; find the locus of the foci,
and, if a tangent be drawn at right angles to $AB$, prove that the locus
of its point of contact $P$ is an hyperbola.

\item[100.] Tangents are drawn from a point $T$ to an hyperbola whose
centre is $C$, and $CT$ produced meets the hyperbola in $P$ and the chord
of contact of the tangents in $V$. If $CD$ be the diameter conjugate to
$CP$, and $DT$, $DV$ meet the tangent at $P$ in $K$ and $U$, prove that the
triangles $PUV$, $TPK$ are equal in area.

\item[101.] One asymptote and three points $P$, $Q$, $R$ of an hyperbola are
given, construct the other asymptote.

\item[102.] If an ellipse be described having its centre on a given
hyperbola, its foci on the asymptotes, and passing through the centre
of the hyperbola, prove that the minor axis of the ellipse is equal to
the major axis of the hyperbola, and the ellipse touches the minor axis
of the hyperbola.

\item[103.] The angular point $A$ of a triangle $ABC$ is fixed, and the
angle $A$ is given, while the points $B$ and $C$ move on a fixed straight
line; prove that the locus of the centre of the circle circumscribing the
triangle is an hyperbola, and that the envelope of the circle is another
circle.

\item[104.] Given an asymptote $CQ$ and two points on an hyperbola,
$P$, $p$ on the curve, shew that the envelope of the axes is a parabola.
%-----File: 143.png-----

\item[105.] Find the locus of the middle points of a system of chords of
an hyperbola, passing through a fixed point on one of the asymptotes.

\item[106.] If a conic be described having for its axes the tangent and
normal at any point of a given ellipse, and touching at its centre the
axis-major of the given ellipse, and if another conic be described in
the same manner, but touching the minor axis at the centre, prove
that the foci of these conics lie in two circles concentric with the
given ellipse, and having their diameters equal to the sum and
difference of its axes.

\item[107.] An ellipse and an hyperbola are confocal; if a tangent to
one intersect at right angles a tangent to the other, the locus of the
point of intersection is a circle.

Shew also that the difference of the squares on the distances from
the centre of parallel tangents is constant.

\item[108.] If a circle passing through any point $P$ of the curve, and
having its centre on the normal at $P$, meets the curve again in $Q$ and
$R$, the tangents at $Q$ and $R$ intersect on a fixed straight line.

\item[109.] If the tangent at $P$ meet an asymptote in $T$, the angle
between that asymptote and $S'P$ is double the angle $STP$.

\item[110.] Four tangents to an hyperbola form a rectangle. If one side
$AB$ of the rectangle intersect a directrix in $F$, and $S$ be the corresponding
focus, the triangles $FSA$, $FBS$ are similar.

\item[111.] An ellipse and hyperbola have the same transverse axis,
and their eccentricities are the reciprocals of one another; prove that
the tangents to each through the focus of the other intersect at right
angles in two points and also meet the conjugate axis on the auxiliary
circle.

\item[112.] $ACA'$ and $BCB'$ are the transverse and conjugate axes of an
ellipse, of which $S$ and $S'$ are the foci. $P$ is one of the points of
intersection of this ellipse and a confocal hyperbola, and $aCa'$ is the
transverse axis of the hyperbola.

Prove that     $SP=Aa$, $S'P=A'a$, and $aB=CP$.

\item[113.] Prove that if $A$, $B$ and $S$ are three given points, two parabolas
can be drawn through $A$ and $B$ with $S$ as focus, and that the axes of
these parabolas are parallel to the asymptotes of the hyperbola which
can be drawn through $S$ with its foci at $A$ and $B$.

\end{exampleslist}
%-----File: 144.png-----

\cchapter{CHAPTER V}

\csectiontoc{The Rectangular Hyperbola}
\cheader{THE RECTANGULAR HYPERBOLA}

\textit{If the axes of an hyperbola be equal, the angle between the
asymptotes is a right angle, and the curve is called equilateral
or rectangular.}

\carticleplus{135.}{Prop.~I\@.} \textit{In a rectangular hyperbola}%\\
\[
CS^2 = 2AC^2, \text{ and }SA^2 = 2AX^2.
\]
\indent For \hfill $CS^2 = AC^2 + BC^2 = 2AC^2$,\hfill\phantom{For\indent}\\
and \hfill $SA : AX :: SC : AC$; \hfill\phantom{and}
\[
\therefore SA^2 = 2AX^2.
\]

Observe that, in the figure of Art.~\cartref{102}, $SDC$ is an isosceles
triangle, since
\[
SD=BC, \text{ and } CD=AC,
\]
and therefore \hfill $SD = DC$. \hfill\phantom{and therefore}

\carticleplus{136.}{Prop.~II\@.} \textit{The asymptotes of a rectangular hyperbola
bisect the angles between any pair of conjugate diameters.}

For, in a rectangular or equilateral hyperbola,
\begin{flalign*}
&& CA &= CB,\\
&\text{and therefore, since}& CP^2 - CD^2 & =CA^2 - CB^2,
    &\phantom{and therefore, since} \\
&& CP &= CD,
\end{flalign*}
$CP$, $CD$ being any conjugate semi-diameters.
%-----File: 145.png-----

Also, in the figure of Art.~\cartref{123}, the parallelogram $CPLD$
is a rhombus, and therefore $CL$ bisects the angle $PCD$.

\subparagraph{Cor.} Supplemental chords are equally inclined to the
asymptotes, for they are parallel to conjugate diameters.

\includegraphicsright[16]{137a}

\carticleplus{137.}{Prop.~III\@.} \textit{If $CY$ be the perpendicular from the
centre on the tangent at $P$, the angle $PCY$ is bisected by the
transverse axis, and half the transverse axis is a mean proportional
between $CY$ and $CP$.}

\begin{flalign*}
\indent\text{For the angle } PCL &= DCL   && \\
                                 &= YCL'; && \\
             \therefore PCA &= ACY.  &&
\end{flalign*}

Hence it follows that the
triangles $PCN$, $TCY$ are similar,
and that
\begin{gather*}
CY:CT::CN:CP; \\
\therefore CY \ctrdot CP=CT \ctrdot CN = AC^2.
\end{gather*}

Hence also, if we join $PA$
and $AY$, we observe that the
triangles $PAC$, $AYC$ are
similar.

\carticleplus{138.}{Prop.~IV\@.} \textit{Diameters at right angles to each other
are equal.}

Let $CP$, $CP'$ be semi-diameters at right angles to each
other, and $CD$ conjugate to $CP$.

Then, if $CL$, $CL'$ be the asymptotes,
\begin{gather*}
\text{the angle } P'CL' = PCL = DCL; \\
\therefore CP' = CD = CP.
\end{gather*}

Hence it follows, by help of the theorem of Art.~\cartref{120}, that
focal chords at right angles to each other are equal, and that
focal chords parallel to conjugate diameters are equal.
%-----File: 146.png-----

\carticleplus{139.}{Prop.~V\@.} \textit{If the normal at $P$ meet the axes in $G$
and $g$,}
\[
CN=NG  \text{ and } PG=Pg=CD,
\]
\textit{$CD$ being conjugate to $CP$.}

\indent{For (Art.~\cartref{115})}  $NG: CN :: BC^2 : AC^2$;
\[ \therefore NG = CN. \]
\indent{Also} \quad $PF \ctrdot PG= BC^2$  and  $PF \ctrdot Pg = AC^2$;
\[ \therefore PG = Pg. \]
\indent{Further (Art.~\cartref{128})} $PG: CD:: BC: AC$;
\[ \therefore PG = CD = CP. \]

\carticleplus{140.}{Prop.~VI\@.} \textit{If $QV$ be an ordinate of a diameter
$PCp$,}
\[
QV^2 = PV \ctrdot Vp.
\]
\begin{flalign*}
\indent & \text{For }&   QV^2 : PV \ctrdot Vp &:: CD^2 : CP^2,&&\\
 \text{and }&&                   CD &= CP;&&\\
&& QV^2 = PV \ctrdot Vp &= CV^2 - CP^2.&&
\end{flalign*}

\carticleplus{141.}{Prop.~VII\@.} \textit{The angle between a chord $PQ$, and
the tangent at $P$, is equal to the angle subtended by $PQ$ at the
other extremity of the diameter through $P$.}

\includegraphicsmid{141a}

Let $PQ$ and the tangent at $P$ meet the asymptote in $l$
and $L$. Then, if $CV$ be conjugate to $PQ$,
\begin{align*}
\text{the angle } LPQ &= PLC-VlC=LCP-VCl&&\\
&= VCP = QpP.&&
\end{align*}
%-----File: 147.png-----

\begin{small}
Or thus, let $QU$ parallel to the tangent at $P$, meet $CP$ produced
in $U$.

Then
\[
QU^2 = PU \ctrdot Up,
\]
or,
\[
QU: PU:: Up: UQ.
\]

Therefore the triangles $PQU$, $QUp$ are similar, and the angle
$QpU = PQU = LPQ$.

\end{small}

If $P$ and $Q$ are on opposite branches of the curve, the
same proof shews that
\[
\text{the angle } QpU= UQP = LPQ;
\]
\[
\therefore QPL' = QpP.
\]

\includegraphicsmid{141b}

If $QP$ is the normal at $P$, it follows that $QP$ subtends a
right angle at the other end of the diameter through $P$.

\carticleplus{142.}{Prop.~VIII\@.} \textit{Any chord subtends, at the ends of
any diameter, angles which are equal or supplementary.}

{\stretchyspace
\includegraphicsright[8]{142a}

This theorem divides itself
into four cases, which are
shewn in the appended figures.

Let $QR$ be the chord, and
$Pp$ the diameter. Then, if $LP$
be the tangent at $P$, fig.~(1),
\begin{flalign*}
\text{the angle}\quad LPQ &= QpP,\\
\text{and}\quad LPR&=RpP;\\
\therefore QPR &= QpR.
\end{flalign*}
%-----File: 148.png-----
In fig. (2), if $pl$ be the tangent at $p$, parallel to $PL$,
\[
QpR = Qpl + lpR = Qpl + pPR,
\]
\includegraphicsmid{142b}
\begin{align*}
\text{and }\qquad\qquad QPR = QPL + LPR& = QpP + LPR;\\
\therefore QpR + QPR =&lpP + LPp,
\end{align*}
that is, $QpR$ and $QPR$ are together equal to two right
angles.
} %end \stretchyspace

\includegraphicsright[8]{142c}

In fig. (3)
\begin{align*}
QPR &= QPL + LPp + pPR\\
&= QpP + Ppl + lpR\\
&= QpR.
\end{align*}

\begin{align*}
\text{In fig. (4) } QPL &= QpP, \text{ and } RPL' = RpP;\\
&\therefore QpR = QPL + RPL';
\end{align*}
therefore $QpR$ and $QPR$ are together
equal to two right angles.

\includegraphicsright[9]{142d}

Hence it will be seen that
when $QR$, or $QR$ produced, meet
the diameter $Pp$ between $P$ and
$p$, the angles subtended at $P$ and
$p$ are equal; in other cases they
are supplementary.

In the cases of the second and
third figures, if one of the angles $QPR$ is a right angle,
the other angle $QpR$ is also a right angle. The four points
$Q$, $P$, $p$, $R$ are then concyclic, and $QR$ is a diameter of the
circle.
%-----File: 149.png-----

\carticleplus{143.}{Prop.~IX\@.} \textit{If a rectangular hyperbola circumscribe
a triangle, it passes through the orthocentre.}

\subparagraph{Note.} \textit{The orthocentre is the point of intersection of the
perpendiculars from the angular points on the opposite sides.}

{\stretchyspace%
\includegraphicsright[8]{143a}

If $O$ be the orthocentre, the
triangles $LOP$, $LQR$ are similar,
and
\begin{flalign*}
LO : LP &:: LQ : LR;\\
\therefore LO \ctrdot LR&=LP \ctrdot LQ.
\end{flalign*}

But, if a rectangular hyperbola
pass through $P$, $Q$, $R$, the
diameters parallel to $LR$, $PQ$ are
equal: hence $O$ is a point on the curve.

If the angle $PRQ$ is a right angle, the line $ROL$ will be
the tangent to the curve at $R$, so that if a rectangular
hyperbola pass through the angular points of a right-angled
triangle, the hypotenuse will be parallel to the normal at the
right-angle vertex.} %end stretchyspace

\carticleplus{144.}{Prop.~X\@.} \textit{If a rectangular hyperbola circumscribe
a triangle, the locus of its centre is the nine-point circle of the
triangle.}

\includegraphicsmid{144a}

If $PQR$ be the triangle, let $L$, $L'$ be the points in which
an asymptote meets the sides $PQ$, $PR$.
%-----File: 150.png-----

Join $C$, the centre of the hyperbola, with $E$ and $F$, the
middle points of $PR$ and $PQ$.

Then $CF$ is conjugate to $PQ$, and $CE$ to $PR$; therefore
the angle
\begin{align*}
FCE &= FCL + L'CE = CLF+ EL'C\\
&= PLL' + PL'L = FPE\\
&=FDE,
\end{align*}
if $D$ be the middle point of $QR$.

$\therefore D$, $E$, $F$, $C$ are concyclic; that is, $C$ lies on the nine-point
circle.

A similar proof is applicable to the case in which the
points $P$, $Q$, $R$ lie on the same branch of the hyperbola.

\csubsection{EXAMPLES}

\begin{exampleslist}
\item[1.] $PCP$ is a transverse diameter, and $QV$ an ordinate; shew that
$QV$ is the tangent at $Q$ to the circle circumscribing the triangle $PQp$.
\cheader{EXAMPLES}

\item[2.] If the tangent at $P$ meet the asymptotes in $L$ and $M$, and the
normal meet the transverse axis in $G$, a circle can be drawn through
$C$, $L$, $M$, and $G$, and $LGM$ is a right angle.

\item[3.] If $AA'$ be any diameter of a circle, $PP'$ any ordinate to it, then
the locus of the intersections of $AP$, $A'P'$ is a rectangular hyperbola.

\item[4.] Given an asymptote and a tangent at a given point, construct
the rectangular hyperbola.

\item[5.] The points of intersection of an ellipse and a confocal rectangular
hyperbola are the extremities of the equi-conjugate diameters of the
ellipse.

\item[6.] If $CP$, $CD$ be conjugate semi-diameters, and $PN$, $DM$ ordinates
of any diameter, the triangles $PCN$, $DCM$ are equal in all respects.

\item[7.] The distance of any point from the centre is a geometric mean
between its distances from the foci.
%-----File: 151.png-----

\item[8.] If $P$ be a point on an equilateral hyperbola, and if the tangent
at $Q$ meet $CP$ in $T$, the circle circumscribing $CTQ$ touches the ordinate
$QV$ conjugate to $CP$.

\item[9.] If a circle be described on $SS'$ as diameter, the tangents at the
vertices will intersect the asymptotes in the circumference.

\item[10.] If two concentric rectangular hyperbolas be described, the
axes of one being the asymptotes of the other, they will intersect at
right angles.

\item[11.] If the tangents at two points $Q$ and $Q'$ meet in $T$, and if $CQ$,
$CQ'$ meet these tangents in $R$ and $R'$, the points $R$, $T$, $R'$, $C$ are concyclic.

\item[12.] If from a point $Q$ in the conjugate axis $QA$ be drawn to the
vertex, and $QR$ parallel to the transverse axis to meet the curve,
$QR=AQ$.

\item[13.] Straight lines, passing through a given point, are bounded by
two fixed lines at right angles to each other; find the locus of their
middle points.

\item[14.] Given a point $Q$ and a straight line $AB$, if a line $QCP$ be
drawn cutting $AB$ in $C$, and $P$ be taken in it, so that, $PD$ being a
perpendicular upon $AB$, $CD$ may be of constant magnitude, the locus
